<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_copper" title="Template:Infobox copper"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_copper" title="Template talk:Infobox copper"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_copper&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Copper</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Cu</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">cuprum</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 29. It is a soft, malleable, and <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a> metal with very high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>. A freshly exposed surface of pure copper has a <a href="/wiki/Copper_(color)" title="Copper (color)">pinkish-orange color</a>. Copper is used as a conductor of heat and electricity, as a <a href="/wiki/Building_material#Metal" title="Building material">building material</a>, and as a constituent of various metal <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">sterling silver</a> used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewelry" title="Jewelry">jewelry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cupronickel" title="Cupronickel">cupronickel</a> used to make marine hardware and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coins" title="Coins">coins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Constantan" title="Constantan">constantan</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Strain_gauge" title="Strain gauge">strain gauges</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermocouples" title="Thermocouples">thermocouples</a> for temperature measurement.
</p><p>Copper is one of the few metals that can occur in nature in a directly usable metallic form (<a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">native metals</a>). This led to very early human use in several regions, from c. 8000 BC. Thousands of years later, it was the first metal to be <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelted</a> from sulfide ores, c. 5000 BC, the first metal to be cast into a shape in a mold, c. 4000 BC and the first metal to be purposefully alloyed with another metal, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, to create <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, c. 3500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncBrit_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncBrit-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman era</a>, copper was principally mined on <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, the origin of the name of the metal, from <i>aes сyprium</i> (metal of Cyprus), later corrupted to <i>сuprum</i> (Latin), from which the words derived, <i>coper</i> (<a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>) and <i>copper</i>, first used around 1530.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The commonly encountered compounds are copper(II) salts, which often impart blue or green colors to such minerals as <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malachite" title="Malachite">malachite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turquoise" title="Turquoise">turquoise</a>, and have been used widely and historically as pigments.
</p><p>Copper used in buildings, usually for roofing, oxidizes to form a green <a href="/wiki/Verdigris" title="Verdigris">verdigris</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patina</a>). Copper is sometimes used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decorative_art" title="Decorative art">decorative art</a>, both in its elemental metal form and in compounds as pigments. Copper compounds are used as <a href="/wiki/Bacteriostatic_agent" title="Bacteriostatic agent">bacteriostatic agents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a>, and wood preservatives.
</p><p>Copper is essential to all living organisms as a trace <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">dietary mineral</a> because it is a key constituent of the respiratory enzyme complex <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molluscs" title="Molluscs">molluscs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">crustaceans</a>, copper is a constituent of the blood pigment <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a>, replaced by the iron-complexed <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> in fish and other <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>. In humans, copper is found mainly in the liver, muscle, and bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The adult body contains between 1.4 and 2.1 mg of copper per kilogram of body weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cu-Scheibe.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3584" data-file-width="3616" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cu-Scheibe.JPG/150px-Cu-Scheibe.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cu-Scheibe.JPG/225px-Cu-Scheibe.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cu-Scheibe.JPG/300px-Cu-Scheibe.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cu-Scheibe.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A copper disc (99.95% pure) made by <a href="/wiki/Continuous_casting" title="Continuous casting">continuous casting</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_etching" title="Industrial etching">etched</a> to reveal <a href="/wiki/Crystallite" title="Crystallite">crystallites</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_just_above_its_melting_point.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="331" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Copper_just_above_its_melting_point.jpg/150px-Copper_just_above_its_melting_point.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Copper_just_above_its_melting_point.jpg/225px-Copper_just_above_its_melting_point.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Copper_just_above_its_melting_point.jpg/300px-Copper_just_above_its_melting_point.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_just_above_its_melting_point.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper just above its melting point keeps its pink luster color when enough light outshines the orange <a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">incandescence</a> color</div></div></div>
<p>Copper, <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> are in <a href="/wiki/Group_11_element" title="Group 11 element">group 11</a> of the periodic table; these three metals have one s-orbital electron on top of a filled d-<a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">electron shell</a> and are characterized by high <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductility</a>, and electrical and thermal conductivity. The filled d-shells in these elements contribute little to interatomic interactions, which are dominated by the s-electrons through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metallic_bond" title="Metallic bond">metallic bonds</a>. Unlike metals with incomplete d-shells, metallic bonds in copper are lacking a <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a> character and are relatively weak. This observation explains the low <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a> and high ductility of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monocrystalline" title="Monocrystalline">single crystals</a> of copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-b1-8">[8]</a></sup> At the macroscopic scale, introduction of extended defects to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystal_lattice" title="Crystal lattice">crystal lattice</a>, such as grain boundaries, hinders flow of the material under applied stress, thereby increasing its hardness. For this reason, copper is usually supplied in a fine-grained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycrystalline" title="Polycrystalline">polycrystalline</a> form, which has greater strength than monocrystalline forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The softness of copper partly explains its high electrical conductivity (59.6×10<sup>6</sup> <a href="/wiki/Siemens_(unit)" title="Siemens (unit)">S</a>/m) and high thermal conductivity, second highest (second only to silver) among pure metals at room temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-10">[10]</a></sup> This is because the resistivity to electron transport in metals at room temperature originates primarily from scattering of electrons on thermal vibrations of the lattice, which are relatively weak in a soft metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-b1-8">[8]</a></sup> The maximum permissible current density of copper in open air is approximately 3.1×10<sup>6</sup> A/m<sup>2</sup> of cross-sectional area, above which it begins to heat excessively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper is one of a few metallic elements with a natural color other than gray or silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Pure copper is orange-red and acquires a reddish <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnish</a> when exposed to air. The characteristic color of copper results from the electronic transitions between the filled 3d and half-empty 4s atomic shells – the energy difference between these shells corresponds to orange light.
</p><p>As with other metals, if copper is put in contact with another metal, <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_corrosion" title="Galvanic corrosion">galvanic corrosion</a> will occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_wire_comparison.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="285" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Copper_wire_comparison.JPG/150px-Copper_wire_comparison.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Copper_wire_comparison.JPG/225px-Copper_wire_comparison.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Copper_wire_comparison.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_wire_comparison.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unoxidized copper wire (left) and oxidized copper wire (right)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="801" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg/220px-Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg/330px-Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg/440px-Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The East Tower of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Edinburgh" title="Royal Observatory, Edinburgh">Royal Observatory, Edinburgh</a>. The contrast between the refurbished copper installed in 2010 and the green color of the original 1894 copper is clearly seen.</div></div></div>
<p>Copper does not react with water, but it does slowly react with atmospheric oxygen to form a layer of brown-black copper oxide which, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rust</a> that forms on iron in moist air, protects the underlying metal from further corrosion (<a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>). A green layer of <a href="/wiki/Verdigris" title="Verdigris">verdigris</a> (copper carbonate) can often be seen on old copper structures, such as the roofing of many older buildings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[14]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Copper <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishes</a> when exposed to some <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> compounds, with which it reacts to form various <a href="/wiki/Copper_sulfide" title="Copper sulfide">copper sulfides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_copper" title="Isotopes of copper">Isotopes of copper</a></div>
<p>There are 29 <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of copper. <sup>63</sup>Cu and <sup>65</sup>Cu are stable, with <sup>63</sup>Cu comprising approximately 69% of naturally occurring copper; both have a <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> of ​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-17">[17]</a></sup> The other isotopes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactive</a>, with the most stable being <sup>67</sup>Cu with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 61.83 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-17">[17]</a></sup> Seven <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">metastable isotopes</a> have been characterized; <sup>68m</sup>Cu is the longest-lived with a half-life of 3.8 minutes. Isotopes with a <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> above 64 decay by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">β<sup>−</sup></a>, whereas those with a mass number below 64 decay by <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">β<sup>+</sup></a>. <a href="/wiki/Copper-64" title="Copper-64"><sup>64</sup>Cu</a>, which has a half-life of 12.7 hours, decays both ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p><sup>62</sup>Cu and <sup>64</sup>Cu have significant applications. <sup>62</sup>Cu is used in <sup>62</sup>Cu-PTSM as a <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracer</a> for <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission_tomography" title="Positron emission tomography">positron emission tomography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_copper_ores" title="List of copper ores">Copper ores</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg/150px-Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg/225px-Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg/300px-Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Native copper from the Keweenaw Peninsula, Michigan, about 2.5 inches (6.4 cm) long</div></div></div>
<p>Copper is produced in massive stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and is present in the Earth's crust in a proportion of about 50 parts per million (ppm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">[21]</a></sup> In nature, copper occurs in a variety of minerals, including <a href="/wiki/Native_copper" title="Native copper">native copper</a>, copper sulfides such as <a href="/wiki/Chalcopyrite" title="Chalcopyrite">chalcopyrite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bornite" title="Bornite">bornite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Digenite" title="Digenite">digenite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Covellite" title="Covellite">covellite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chalcocite" title="Chalcocite">chalcocite</a>, copper <a href="/wiki/Sulfosalt_minerals" title="Sulfosalt minerals">sulfosalts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedrite" title="Tetrahedrite">tetrahedite-tennantite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Enargite" title="Enargite">enargite</a>, copper carbonates such as <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malachite" title="Malachite">malachite</a>, and as copper(I) or copper(II) oxides such as <a href="/wiki/Cuprite" title="Cuprite">cuprite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tenorite" title="Tenorite">tenorite</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-10">[10]</a></sup>  The largest mass of elemental copper discovered weighed 420 tonnes and was found in 1857 on the <a href="/wiki/Keweenaw_Peninsula" title="Keweenaw Peninsula">Keweenaw Peninsula</a> in Michigan, US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">[21]</a></sup> Native copper is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycrystal" title="Polycrystal">polycrystal</a>, with the largest single crystal ever described measuring 4.4×3.2×3.2 cm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chuquicamata-002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1369" data-file-width="2126" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chuquicamata-002.jpg/220px-Chuquicamata-002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chuquicamata-002.jpg/330px-Chuquicamata-002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chuquicamata-002.jpg/440px-Chuquicamata-002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chuquicamata-002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chuquicamata" title="Chuquicamata">Chuquicamata</a>, in Chile, is one of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">open pit</a> copper <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mines</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="750" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg/220px-Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg/330px-Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg/440px-Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World production trend</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Price_History_USD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="402" data-file-width="934" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Copper_Price_History_USD.png/220px-Copper_Price_History_USD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Copper_Price_History_USD.png/330px-Copper_Price_History_USD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Copper_Price_History_USD.png/440px-Copper_Price_History_USD.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Price_History_USD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper prices 2003–2011 in US$ per tonne</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_copper_production" title="List of countries by copper production">List of countries by copper production</a></div>
<p>Most copper is mined or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_extraction_techniques" title="Copper extraction techniques">extracted</a> as copper sulfides from large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_pit_mine" title="Open pit mine">open pit mines</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porphyry_copper" title="Porphyry copper">porphyry copper</a> deposits that contain 0.4 to 1.0% copper. Sites include <a href="/wiki/Chuquicamata" title="Chuquicamata">Chuquicamata</a>, in Chile, <a href="/wiki/Bingham_Canyon_Mine" title="Bingham Canyon Mine">Bingham Canyon Mine</a>, in Utah, United States, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Chino_Mine" title="El Chino Mine">El Chino Mine</a>, in New Mexico, United States. According to the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a>, in 2005, Chile was the top producer of copper with at least one-third of the world share followed by the United States, Indonesia and Peru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-10">[10]</a></sup> Copper can also be recovered through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_leach" title="In-situ leach">in-situ leach</a> process. Several sites in the state of Arizona are considered prime candidates for this method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The amount of copper in use is increasing and the quantity available is barely sufficient to allow all countries to reach developed world levels of usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reserves">Reserves</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Peak_copper#Reserves" title="Peak copper">Peak copper § Reserves</a></div>
<p>Copper has been in use at least 10,000 years, but more than 95% of all copper ever mined and <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelted</a> has been extracted since 1900,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard2006_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard2006-25">[25]</a></sup> and more than half was extracted the last 24 years. As with many natural resources, the total amount of copper on Earth is vast, with around 10<sup>14</sup> tons in the top kilometer of Earth's crust, which is about 5 million years' worth at the current rate of extraction. However, only a tiny fraction of these reserves is economically viable with present-day prices and technologies. Estimates of copper reserves available for mining vary from 25 to 60 years, depending on core assumptions such as the growth rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Recycling is a major source of copper in the modern world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard2006_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard2006-25">[25]</a></sup> Because of these and other factors, the future of copper production and supply is the subject of much debate, including the concept of <a href="/wiki/Peak_copper" title="Peak copper">peak copper</a>, analogous to <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>.
</p><p>The price of copper has historically been unstable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and its price increased from the 60-year low of US$0.60/lb (US$1.32/kg) in June 1999 to $3.75 per pound ($8.27/kg) in May 2006. It dropped to $2.40/lb ($5.29/kg) in February 2007, then rebounded to $3.50/lb ($7.71/kg) in April 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This page is useless for verification as it is updated hourly. Archives exist, but they do not go back far enough and in any case the graphs are not shown with the precision quoted (April 2014)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In February 2009, weakening global demand and a steep fall in commodity prices since the previous year's highs left copper prices at $1.51/lb ($3.32/kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">Methods</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_extraction_techniques" title="Copper extraction techniques">Copper extraction techniques</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_(EN).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="980" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg/220px-Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg/330px-Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg/440px-Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_(EN).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scheme of flash smelting process</div></div></div>
<p>The concentration of copper in ores averages only 0.6%, and most commercial ores are sulfides, especially chalcopyrite (CuFeS<sub>2</sub>), bornite (Cu<sub>5</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>) and, to a lesser extent, covellite (CuS) and chalcocite (Cu<sub>2</sub>S).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G&amp;E_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-G&amp;E-30">[30]</a></sup> These minerals are concentrated from <a href="/wiki/Comminution" title="Comminution">crushed</a> ores to the level of 10–15% copper by <a href="/wiki/Froth_flotation" title="Froth flotation">froth flotation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bioleaching" title="Bioleaching">bioleaching</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Heating this material with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a> in <a href="/wiki/Flash_smelting" title="Flash smelting">flash smelting</a> removes much of the iron as <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a>. The process exploits the greater ease of converting iron sulfides into oxides, which in turn react with the silica to form the <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> slag that floats on top of the heated mass. The resulting <i>copper matte,</i> consisting of Cu<sub>2</sub>S, is <a href="/wiki/Roasting_(metallurgy)" title="Roasting (metallurgy)">roasted</a> to convert all sulfides into oxides:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G&amp;E_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-G&amp;E-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Cu<sub>2</sub>S + 3 O<sub>2</sub>  → 2 Cu<sub>2</sub>O + 2 SO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The cuprous oxide is converted to <i>blister</i> copper upon heating:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Cu<sub>2</sub>O → 4 Cu + O<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The Sudbury <a href="/wiki/Matte_(metallurgy)" title="Matte (metallurgy)">matte</a> process converted only half the sulfide to oxide and then used this oxide to remove the rest of the sulfur as oxide. It was then electrolytically refined and the anode mud exploited for the <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> and gold it contained. This step exploits the relatively easy reduction of copper oxides to copper metal. <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">Natural gas</a> is blown across the blister to remove most of the remaining oxygen and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrorefining" title="Electrorefining">electrorefining</a> is performed on the resulting material to produce pure copper:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Cu<sup>2+</sup> + 2 e<sup>−</sup> → Cu</dd></dl>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumbinner" style="background:none;border:none;max-width:px;">
<div class="floatnone"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ural_Mining_and_Metallurgical_Company_Copper_Map.svg" title="Flowchart of copper refining (Anode casting plant of Uralelektromed) # Blister copper # Smelting # Reverberatory furnace # Slag removal # Copper casting of anodes # Casting wheel # Anodes removal machine # Anodes take-off # Rail cars # Transportation to the tank house"><img alt="Flowchart of copper refining (Anode casting plant of Uralelektromed) # Blister copper # Smelting # Reverberatory furnace # Slag removal # Copper casting of anodes # Casting wheel # Anodes removal machine # Anodes take-off # Rail cars # Transportation to the tank house" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="797" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ural_Mining_and_Metallurgical_Company_Copper_Map.svg/797px-Ural_Mining_and_Metallurgical_Company_Copper_Map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ural_Mining_and_Metallurgical_Company_Copper_Map.svg/1196px-Ural_Mining_and_Metallurgical_Company_Copper_Map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Ural_Mining_and_Metallurgical_Company_Copper_Map.svg/1594px-Ural_Mining_and_Metallurgical_Company_Copper_Map.svg.png 2x" width="797"/></a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling">Recycling</span></h3>
<p>Like <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> copper is recyclable without any loss of quality, both from raw state and from manufactured products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In volume, copper is the third most recycled metal after iron and aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> An estimated 80% of all copper ever mined is still in use today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Metal_Stocks_in_Society_report" title="Metal Stocks in Society report">Metal Stocks in Society report</a>, the global per capita stock of copper in use in society is 35–55 kg. Much of this is in more-developed countries (140–300 kg per capita) rather than less-developed countries (30–40 kg per capita).
</p><p>The process of recycling copper is roughly the same as is used to extract copper but requires fewer steps. High-purity scrap copper is melted in a <a href="/wiki/Furnace#Metallurgical_furnace" title="Furnace">furnace</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduced</a> and cast into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billet_(semi-finished_product)" title="Billet (semi-finished product)">billets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ingot" title="Ingot">ingots</a>; lower-purity scrap is refined by <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> in a bath of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloys">Alloys</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_copper_alloys" title="List of copper alloys">List of copper alloys</a></div>
<p>Numerous copper <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> have been formulated, many with important uses. <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">Brass</a> is an alloy of copper and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">Bronze</a> usually refers to copper-<a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> alloys, but can refer to any alloy of copper such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_bronze" title="Aluminium bronze">aluminium bronze</a>. Copper is one of the most important constituents of silver and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carat_(purity)" title="Carat (purity)">carat</a> gold and carat solders used in the jewelry industry, modifying the color, hardness and melting point of the resulting alloys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldalloys_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldalloys-38">[38]</a></sup> Some lead-free <a href="/wiki/Solder#Solder_alloys" title="Solder">solders</a> consist of tin alloyed with a small proportion of copper and other metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The alloy of copper and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Cupronickel" title="Cupronickel">cupronickel</a>, is used in low-denomination coins, often for the outer cladding. The US five-cent coin (currently called a <i>nickel</i>) consists of 75% copper and 25% nickel in homogeneous composition. The alloy of 90% copper and 10% nickel, remarkable for its resistance to corrosion, is used for various objects exposed to seawater, though it is vulnerable to the sulfides sometimes found in polluted harbors and estuaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Alloys of copper with aluminium (about 7%) have a golden color and are used in decorations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">[21]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Shakud%C5%8D" title="Shakudō">Shakudō</a></i> is a Japanese decorative alloy of copper containing a low percentage of gold, typically 4–10%, that can be <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patinated</a> to a dark blue or black color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shakudō_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shakudō-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CopperIoxide.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="344" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/CopperIoxide.jpg/220px-CopperIoxide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/CopperIoxide.jpg/330px-CopperIoxide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/CopperIoxide.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CopperIoxide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sample of <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_oxide" title="Copper(I) oxide">copper(I) oxide</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Copper_compounds" title="Category:Copper compounds">Category:Copper compounds</a></div>
<p>Copper forms a rich variety of compounds, usually with <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> +1 and +2, which are often called <i>cuprous</i> and <i>cupric</i>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holleman_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holleman-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_compounds">Binary compounds</span></h3>
<p>As with other elements, the simplest compounds of copper are binary compounds, i.e. those containing only two elements, the principal examples being oxides, sulfides, and <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a>. Both <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_oxide" title="Copper(I) oxide">cuprous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_oxide" title="Copper(II) oxide">cupric oxides</a> are known. Among the numerous <a href="/wiki/Copper_sulfide" title="Copper sulfide">copper sulfides</a>, important examples include <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_sulfide" title="Copper(I) sulfide">copper(I) sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper_monosulfide" title="Copper monosulfide">copper(II) sulfide</a>.
</p><p>Cuprous halides (with <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_chloride" title="Copper(I) chloride">chlorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_bromide" title="Copper(I) bromide">bromine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_iodide" title="Copper(I) iodide">iodine</a>) are known, as are cupric halides with <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_fluoride" title="Copper(II) fluoride">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_chloride" title="Copper(II) chloride">chlorine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_bromide" title="Copper(II) bromide">bromine</a>. Attempts to prepare copper(II) iodide yield only cuprous iodide and iodine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holleman_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holleman-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Cu<sup>2+</sup> + 4 I<sup>−</sup> → 2 CuI + I<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_chemistry">Coordination chemistry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:205px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tetramminkupfer(II)-sulfat-Monohydrat_Kristalle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="201" data-file-width="203" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Tetramminkupfer%28II%29-sulfat-Monohydrat_Kristalle.png" width="203"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tetramminkupfer(II)-sulfat-Monohydrat_Kristalle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper(II) gives a deep blue coloration in the presence of ammonia ligands. The one used here is <a href="/wiki/Tetraamminecopper(II)_sulfate" title="Tetraamminecopper(II) sulfate">tetramminecopper(II) sulfate</a>.</div></div></div><p>
Copper forms <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complexes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a>. In aqueous solution, copper(II) exists as [Cu(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>2+</sup>. This complex exhibits the fastest water exchange rate (speed of water ligands attaching and detaching) for any transition <a href="/wiki/Metal_aquo_complex" title="Metal aquo complex">metal aquo complex</a>. Adding aqueous <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> causes the precipitation of light blue solid <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_hydroxide" title="Copper(II) hydroxide">copper(II) hydroxide</a>. A simplified equation is: </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="420" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg/220px-Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg/330px-Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg/440px-Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pourbaix diagram for copper in uncomplexed media (anions other than OH- not considered). Ion concentration 0.001 m (mol/kg water). Temperature 25 °C.</div></div></div>
<dl><dd>Cu<sup>2+</sup> + 2 OH<sup>−</sup> → Cu(OH)<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ammonia_solution" title="Ammonia solution">Aqueous ammonia</a> results in the same precipitate. Upon adding excess ammonia, the precipitate dissolves, forming <a href="/wiki/Schweizer%27s_reagent" title="Schweizer's reagent">tetraamminecopper(II)</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>Cu(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>2</sub> + 4 NH<sub>3</sub> → [Cu(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>2+</sup> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O + 2 OH<sup>−</sup></dd></dl>
<p>Many other <a href="/wiki/Oxyanion" title="Oxyanion">oxyanions</a> form complexes; these include <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_acetate" title="Copper(II) acetate">copper(II) acetate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_nitrate" title="Copper(II) nitrate">copper(II) nitrate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_carbonate" title="Copper(II) carbonate">copper(II) carbonate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_sulfate" title="Copper(II) sulfate">Copper(II) sulfate</a> forms a blue crystalline penta<a href="/wiki/Hydrate" title="Hydrate">hydrate</a>, the most familiar copper compound in the laboratory. It is used in a <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicide</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux_mixture" title="Bordeaux mixture">Bordeaux mixture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boux_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boux-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tetraamminediaquacopper(II)-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1100" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/200px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/300px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/400px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tetraamminediaquacopper(II)-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model" title="Ball-and-stick model">Ball-and-stick model</a> of the complex [Cu(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>2+</sup>, illustrating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octahedral_coordination_geometry" title="Octahedral coordination geometry">octahedral coordination geometry</a> common for copper(II).</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polyol" title="Polyol">Polyols</a>, compounds containing more than one alcohol <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional group</a>, generally interact with cupric salts. For example, copper salts are used to test for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reducing_sugars" title="Reducing sugars">reducing sugars</a>. Specifically, using <a href="/wiki/Benedict%27s_reagent" title="Benedict's reagent">Benedict's reagent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fehling%27s_solution" title="Fehling's solution">Fehling's solution</a> the presence of the sugar is signaled by a color change from blue Cu(II) to reddish copper(I) oxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Schweizer's reagent and related complexes with <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine" title="Ethylenediamine">ethylenediamine</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a> dissolve <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">Amino acids</a> form very stable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chelate_complex" title="Chelate complex">chelate complexes</a> with copper(II). Many wet-chemical tests for copper ions exist, one involving <a href="/wiki/Potassium_ferrocyanide" title="Potassium ferrocyanide">potassium ferrocyanide</a>, which gives a brown precipitate with copper(II) salts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organocopper_chemistry">Organocopper chemistry</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organocopper_compound" title="Organocopper compound">Organocopper compound</a></div>
<p>Compounds that contain a carbon-copper bond are known as organocopper compounds. They are very reactive towards oxygen to form copper(I) oxide and have <a href="/wiki/Reactions_of_organocopper_reagents" title="Reactions of organocopper reagents">many uses in chemistry</a>. They are synthesized by treating copper(I) compounds with <a href="/wiki/Grignard_reaction" title="Grignard reaction">Grignard reagents</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminal_alkyne" title="Terminal alkyne">terminal alkynes</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organolithium_compound" title="Organolithium compound">organolithium reagents</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> in particular, the last reaction described produces a <a href="/wiki/Gilman_reagent" title="Gilman reagent">Gilman reagent</a>. These can undergo <a href="/wiki/Substitution_reaction" title="Substitution reaction">substitution</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_halides" title="Alkyl halides">alkyl halides</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Coupling_reaction" title="Coupling reaction">coupling products</a>; as such, they are important in the field of <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_acetylide" title="Copper(I) acetylide">Copper(I) acetylide</a> is highly shock-sensitive but is an intermediate in reactions such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadiot-Chodkiewicz_coupling" title="Cadiot-Chodkiewicz coupling">Cadiot-Chodkiewicz coupling</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Sonogashira_coupling" title="Sonogashira coupling">Sonogashira coupling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_conjugate_addition" title="Nucleophilic conjugate addition">Conjugate addition</a> to <a href="/wiki/Enone" title="Enone">enones</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Carbometalation" title="Carbometalation">carbocupration</a> of alkynes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> can also be achieved with organocopper compounds. Copper(I) forms a variety of weak complexes with <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, especially in the presence of amine ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Copper.28III.29_and_copper.28IV.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Copper(III)_and_copper(IV)">Copper(III) and copper(IV)</span></h3>
<p>Copper(III) is most often found in oxides. A simple example is potassium <a href="/wiki/Cuprate" title="Cuprate">cuprate</a>, KCuO<sub>2</sub>, a blue-black solid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The most extensively studied copper(III) compounds are the <a href="/wiki/Cuprate_superconductor" title="Cuprate superconductor">cuprate superconductors</a>. <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_barium_copper_oxide" title="Yttrium barium copper oxide">Yttrium barium copper oxide</a> (YBa<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) consists of both Cu(II) and Cu(III) centres. Like oxide, <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> is a highly <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and is known to stabilize metal ions in high oxidation states. Both copper(III) and even copper(IV) fluorides are known, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hexafluorocuprate(III)" title="Potassium hexafluorocuprate(III)">K<sub>3</sub>CuF<sub>6</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Caesium_hexafluorocuprate(IV)" title="Caesium hexafluorocuprate(IV)">Cs<sub>2</sub>CuF<sub>6</sub></a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holleman_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Holleman-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some copper proteins form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxo_complex" title="Oxo complex">oxo complexes</a>, which also feature copper(III).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Tetrapeptide" title="Tetrapeptide">tetrapeptides</a>, purple-colored copper(III) complexes are stabilized by the deprotonated <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amide</a> ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Complexes of copper(III) are also found as intermediates in reactions of organocopper compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Kharasch%E2%80%93Sosnovsky_reaction" title="Kharasch–Sosnovsky reaction">Kharasch–Sosnovsky reaction</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>A timeline of copper illustrates how the metal has advanced human civilization for the past 11,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_history">Prehistoric history</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Copper_Age">Copper Age</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_Age" title="Copper Age">Copper Age</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros,_Crete.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="803" data-file-width="1264" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg/220px-Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg/330px-Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg/440px-Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros,_Crete.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A corroded copper <a href="/wiki/Ingot" title="Ingot">ingot</a> from <a href="/wiki/Zakros" title="Zakros">Zakros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, shaped in the form of an animal skin typical in that era.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3126" data-file-width="1980" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg/220px-ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg/330px-ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg/440px-ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many tools during the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> Era included copper, such as the blade of this replica of <a href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi" title="Ötzi">Ötzi</a>'s axe</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1540" data-file-width="2264" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg/220px-Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg/330px-Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg/440px-Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper ore (<a href="/wiki/Chrysocolla" title="Chrysocolla">chrysocolla</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> sandstone from <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> mines in the <a href="/wiki/Timna_Valley" title="Timna Valley">Timna Valley</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Copper occurs naturally as <a href="/wiki/Native_copper" title="Native copper">native metallic copper</a> and was known to some of the oldest civilizations on record. The history of copper use dates to 9000 BC in the Middle East;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovery_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-discovery-58">[58]</a></sup> a copper pendant was found in northern Iraq that dates to 8700 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Evidence suggests that gold and <a href="/wiki/Meteoric_iron" title="Meteoric iron">meteoric iron</a> (but not smelted iron) were the only metals used by humans before copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vander_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-vander-60">[60]</a></sup> The history of copper metallurgy is thought to follow this sequence: First, <a href="/wiki/Work_hardening" title="Work hardening">cold working</a> of native copper, then <a href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">annealing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a>, and, finally,  <a href="/wiki/Lost-wax_casting" title="Lost-wax casting">lost-wax casting</a>. In southeastern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, all four of these techniques appear more or less simultaneously at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> c. 7500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renfrew1990_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renfrew1990-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper smelting was independently invented in different places. It was probably discovered in China before 2800 BC, in Central America around 600 AD, and in West Africa about the 9th or 10th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Investment_casting" title="Investment casting">Investment casting</a> was invented in 4500–4000 BC in Southeast Asia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovery_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-discovery-58">[58]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dating" title="Carbon dating">carbon dating</a> has established mining at <a href="/wiki/Alderley_Edge_Mines" title="Alderley Edge Mines">Alderley Edge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, UK, at 2280 to 1890 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi_the_Iceman" title="Ötzi the Iceman">Ötzi the Iceman</a>, a male dated from 3300–3200 BC, was found with an axe with a copper head 99.7% pure; high levels of <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> in his hair suggest an involvement in copper smelting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSA_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSA-64">[64]</a></sup> Experience with copper has assisted the development of other metals; in particular, copper smelting led to the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Bloomery" title="Bloomery">iron smelting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSA_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-CSA-64">[64]</a></sup> Production in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Copper_Complex" title="Old Copper Complex">Old Copper Complex</a> in Michigan and Wisconsin is dated between 6000 and 3000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-occ_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-occ-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Natural bronze, a type of copper made from ores rich in silicon, arsenic, and (rarely) tin, came into general use in the Balkans around 5500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a></div>
<p>Alloying copper with tin to make bronze was first practiced about 4000 years after the discovery of copper smelting, and about 2000 years after "natural bronze" had come into general use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Bronze artifacts from the <a href="/wiki/Vin%C4%8Da_culture" title="Vinča culture">Vinča culture</a> date to 4500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-antiquity1312_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-antiquity1312-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> artifacts of copper and bronze alloys date to 3000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hist_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-hist-70">[70]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> began in Southeastern Europe around 3700–3300 BC, in Northwestern Europe about 2500 BC. It ended with the beginning of the Iron Age, 2000–1000 BC in the Near East, and 600 BC in Northern Europe. The transition between the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period and the Bronze Age was formerly termed the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> period (copper-stone), when copper tools were used with stone tools. The term has gradually fallen out of favor because in some parts of the world, the Chalcolithic and Neolithic are coterminous at both ends. Brass, an alloy of copper and zinc, is of much more recent origin. It was known to the Greeks, but became a significant supplement to bronze during the Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hist_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-hist-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_Post-classical_history">Ancient and Post-classical history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_symbol.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="75" data-file-width="75" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Venus_symbol.svg/100px-Venus_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Venus_symbol.svg/150px-Venus_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Venus_symbol.svg/200px-Venus_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_symbol.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> the symbol for copper was also the symbol for the goddess and planet <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/220px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/330px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/440px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chalcolithic copper mine in <a href="/wiki/Timna_Valley" title="Timna Valley">Timna Valley</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negev_Desert" title="Negev Desert">Negev Desert</a>, Israel.</div></div></div>
<p>In Greece, copper was known by the name <i>chalkos</i> (χαλκός). It was an important resource for the Romans, Greeks and other ancient peoples. In Roman times, it was known as <i>aes Cyprium</i>, <i>aes</i> being the generic Latin term for copper alloys and <i>Cyprium</i> from <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, where much copper was mined. The phrase was simplified to <i>cuprum</i>, hence the English <i>copper</i>. <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venus_(goddess)" title="Venus (goddess)">Venus</a> in Rome) represented copper in mythology and alchemy because of its lustrous beauty and its ancient use in producing mirrors; Cyprus was sacred to the goddess. The seven heavenly bodies known to the ancients were associated with the seven metals known in antiquity, and Venus was assigned to copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper was first used in ancient Britain in about the 3rd or 2nd Century BC. In North America, copper mining began with marginal workings by Native Americans. Native copper is known to have been extracted from sites on <a href="/wiki/Isle_Royale" title="Isle Royale">Isle Royale</a> with primitive stone tools between 800 and 1600.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Copper metallurgy was flourishing in South America, particularly in Peru around 1000 AD. Copper burial ornamentals from the 15th century have been uncovered, but the metal's commercial production did not start until the early 20th century.
</p><p>The cultural role of copper has been important, particularly in currency. Romans in the 6th through 3rd centuries BC used copper lumps as money. At first, the copper itself was valued, but gradually the shape and look of the copper became more important. <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> had his own coins made from brass, while <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Octavianus Augustus Caesar</a>'s coins were made from Cu-Pb-Sn alloys. With an estimated annual output of around 15,000 t, <a href="/wiki/Roman_metallurgy" title="Roman metallurgy">Roman copper mining and smelting activities</a> reached a scale unsurpassed until the time of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">provinces</a> most intensely mined were those of <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and in Central Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The gates of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jerusalem" title="Temple of Jerusalem">Temple of Jerusalem</a> used <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_bronze" title="Corinthian bronze">Corinthian bronze</a> treated with <a href="/wiki/Depletion_gilding" title="Depletion gilding">depletion gilding</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Bronze is not a gold alloy, but depletion gilding can be done only on gold alloy. (June 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The process was most prevalent in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, where alchemy is thought to have begun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In ancient <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, copper was used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holistic" title="Holistic">holistic</a> medical science <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical" title="Surgical">surgical</a> instruments and other medical equipment. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptians</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom" title="Old Kingdom">~2400 BC</a>) used copper for sterilizing wounds and drinking water, and later to treat headaches, burns, and itching.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AngleseyCopperStream.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="656" data-file-width="492" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/AngleseyCopperStream.jpg/220px-AngleseyCopperStream.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/AngleseyCopperStream.jpg/330px-AngleseyCopperStream.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/AngleseyCopperStream.jpg/440px-AngleseyCopperStream.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AngleseyCopperStream.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Acid_mine_drainage" title="Acid mine drainage">Acid mine drainage</a> affecting the stream running from the disused <a href="/wiki/Parys_Mountain" title="Parys Mountain">Parys Mountain</a> copper mines</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Pot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3608" data-file-width="3850" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Copper_Pot.jpg/220px-Copper_Pot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Copper_Pot.jpg/330px-Copper_Pot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Copper_Pot.jpg/440px-Copper_Pot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Pot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>18th century copper <a href="/wiki/Kettle" title="Kettle">kettle</a> from Norway made from Swedish copper</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Copper_Mountain" title="Great Copper Mountain">Great Copper Mountain</a> was a mine in Falun, Sweden, that operated from the 10th century to 1992. It satisfied two thirds of Europe's copper consumption in the 17th century and helped fund many of Sweden's wars during that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> It was referred to as the nation's treasury; Sweden had a <a href="/wiki/History_of_copper_currency_in_Sweden" title="History of copper currency in Sweden">copper backed currency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper is used in roofing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[14]</a></sup> currency, and for photographic technology known as the <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a>. Copper was used in <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> sculpture, and was used to construct the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a>; copper continues to be used in construction of various types. <a href="/wiki/Copper_plating" title="Copper plating">Copper plating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper_sheathing" title="Copper sheathing">copper sheathing</a> were widely used to protect the under-water hulls of ships, a technique pioneered by the British <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> in the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norddeutsche_Affinerie" title="Norddeutsche Affinerie">Norddeutsche Affinerie</a> in Hamburg was the first modern <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> plant, starting its production in 1876.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The German scientist <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Osann" title="Gottfried Osann">Gottfried Osann</a> invented <a href="/wiki/Powder_metallurgy" title="Powder metallurgy">powder metallurgy</a> in 1830 while determining the metal's atomic mass; around then it was discovered that the amount and type of alloying element (e.g., tin) to copper would affect bell tones. <a href="/wiki/Flash_smelting" title="Flash smelting">Flash smelting</a> was developed by <a href="/wiki/Outokumpu" title="Outokumpu">Outokumpu</a> in Finland and first applied at <a href="/wiki/Harjavalta" title="Harjavalta">Harjavalta</a> in 1949; the energy-efficient process accounts for 50% of the world's primary copper production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Council_of_Copper_Exporting_Countries" title="Intergovernmental Council of Copper Exporting Countries">Intergovernmental Council of Copper Exporting Countries</a>, formed in 1967 by Chile, Peru, Zaire and Zambia, operated in the copper market as <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> does in oil, though it never achieved the same influence, particularly because the second-largest producer, the United States, was never a member; it was dissolved in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_renewable_energy" title="Copper in renewable energy">Copper in renewable energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kupferfittings_4062.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kupferfittings_4062.jpg/220px-Kupferfittings_4062.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kupferfittings_4062.jpg/330px-Kupferfittings_4062.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kupferfittings_4062.jpg/440px-Kupferfittings_4062.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kupferfittings_4062.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Assorted copper fittings</div></div></div>
<p>The major applications of copper are electrical wire (60%), roofing and plumbing (20%), and industrial machinery (15%). Copper is used mostly as a pure metal, but when greater hardness is required, it is put into such alloys as <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> (5% of total use).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-21">[21]</a></sup> For more than two centuries, copper paint has been used on boat hulls to control the growth of plants and shellfish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> A small part of the copper supply is used for nutritional supplements and fungicides in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boux_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boux-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applications_for_Copper_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Applications_for_Copper-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Machining" title="Machining">Machining</a> of copper is possible, although alloys are preferred for good <a href="/wiki/Machinability" title="Machinability">machinability</a> in creating intricate parts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wire_and_cable">Wire and cable</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_wire_and_cable" title="Copper wire and cable">Copper wire and cable</a></div>
<p>Despite competition from other materials, copper remains the preferred <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductor</a> in nearly all categories of electrical wiring except overhead <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">electric power transmission</a> where <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> is often preferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Copper wire is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_generation" title="Power generation">power generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Power_transmission" title="Power transmission">power transmission</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_distribution" title="Power distribution">power distribution</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a> circuitry, and countless types of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_equipment" title="Electrical equipment">electrical equipment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electrical_wiring" title="Electrical wiring">Electrical wiring</a> is the most important market for the copper industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This includes structural power wiring, power distribution cable, appliance wire, communications cable, automotive wire and cable, and magnet wire. Roughly half of all copper mined is used for electrical wire and cable conductors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Many electrical devices rely on copper wiring because of its multitude of inherent beneficial properties, such as its high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">tensile strength</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Creep_(deformation)" title="Creep (deformation)">creep (deformation)</a> resistance, <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> resistance, low <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a>, high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, ease of <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">soldering</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malleability" title="Malleability">malleability</a>, and ease of installation.
</p><p>For a short period from the late 1960s to the late 1970s, copper wiring was replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_wiring" title="Aluminium wiring">aluminium wiring</a> in many housing construction projects in America.  The new wiring was implicated in a number of house fires and the industry returned to copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics_and_related_devices">Electronics and related devices</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Busbars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="640" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Busbars.jpg/220px-Busbars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Busbars.jpg/330px-Busbars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Busbars.jpg/440px-Busbars.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Busbars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper electrical <a href="/wiki/Busbar" title="Busbar">busbars</a> distributing power to a large building</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">Integrated circuits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">printed circuit boards</a> increasingly feature copper in place of aluminium because of its superior electrical conductivity; <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sinks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchangers</a> use copper because of its superior heat dissipation properties. <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">Electromagnets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">vacuum tubes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">cathode ray tubes</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetron" title="Magnetron">magnetrons</a> in microwave ovens use copper, as do <a href="/wiki/Waveguide" title="Waveguide">waveguides</a> for microwave radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_motors">Electric motors</span></h3>
