<p>Copper's superior <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_wire_and_cable#Electrical_conductivity" title="Copper wire and cable">conductivity</a> enhances the efficiency of electrical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motor_(device)" title="Motor (device)">motors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> This is important because motors and motor-driven systems account for 43%–46% of all global electricity consumption and 69% of all electricity used by industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Increasing the mass and cross section of copper in a <a href="/wiki/Inductor" title="Inductor">coil</a> increases the efficiency of the motor. <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">Copper motor rotors</a>, a new technology designed for motor applications where energy savings are prime design objectives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> are enabling general-purpose <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">induction motors</a> to meet and exceed <a href="/wiki/National_Electrical_Manufacturers_Association" title="National Electrical Manufacturers Association">National Electrical Manufacturers Association</a> (NEMA) <a href="/wiki/Premium_efficiency" title="Premium efficiency">premium efficiency</a> standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_architecture" title="Copper in architecture">Copper in architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3200" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg/220px-Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg/330px-Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg/440px-Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper roof on the <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis_City_Hall" title="Minneapolis City Hall">Minneapolis City Hall</a>, coated with <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patina</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="488" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg/220px-Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg/330px-Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg/440px-Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Old copper utensils in a Jerusalem restaurant</div></div></div>
<p>Copper has been used since ancient times as a durable, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrosion_resistance" title="Corrosion resistance">corrosion resistant</a>, and weatherproof architectural material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roofing_material" title="Roofing material">Roofs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flashing_(weatherproofing)" title="Flashing (weatherproofing)">flashings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rain_gutter" title="Rain gutter">rain gutters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Downspout" title="Downspout">downspouts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">domes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">spires</a>, vaults, and <a href="/wiki/Door" title="Door">doors</a> have been made from copper for hundreds or thousands of years. Copper's architectural use has been expanded in modern times to include interior and exterior <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_architecture#Wall_cladding" title="Copper in architecture">wall cladding</a>, building <a href="/wiki/Expansion_joint" title="Expansion joint">expansion joints</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RF_shielding" title="RF shielding">radio frequency shielding</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_copper-alloy_touch_surfaces" title="Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces">antimicrobial</a> and decorative indoor products such as attractive handrails, bathroom fixtures, and counter tops. Some of copper's other important benefits as an architectural material include low <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal movement</a>, light weight, <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning protection</a>, and recyclability.
</p><p>The metal's distinctive natural green <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patina</a> has long been coveted by architects and designers. The final patina is a particularly durable layer that is highly resistant to atmospheric corrosion, thereby protecting the underlying metal against further weathering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> It can be a mixture of carbonate and sulfate compounds in various amounts, depending upon environmental conditions such as sulfur-containing acid rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Architectural copper and its alloys can also be <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_architecture#Finishes" title="Copper in architecture">'finished'</a> to take on a particular look, feel, or color. Finishes include mechanical surface treatments, chemical coloring, and coatings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper has excellent <a href="/wiki/Brazing" title="Brazing">brazing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soldering" title="Soldering">soldering</a> properties and can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welded" title="Welded">welded</a>; the best results are obtained with <a href="/wiki/Gas_metal_arc_welding" title="Gas metal arc welding">gas metal arc welding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antibiofouling_applications">Antibiofouling applications</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Copper_alloys_in_aquaculture" title="Copper alloys in aquaculture">Copper alloys in aquaculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper_sheathing" title="Copper sheathing">Copper sheathing</a></div>
<p>Copper is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biostatic" title="Biostatic">biostatic</a>, meaning bacteria and many other forms of life will not grow on it. For this reason it has long been used to line parts of ships to protect against <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a>. It was originally used pure, but has since been superseded by <a href="/wiki/Muntz_metal" title="Muntz metal">Muntz metal</a> and copper-based paint. Similarly, as discussed in <a href="/wiki/Copper_alloys_in_aquaculture" title="Copper alloys in aquaculture">copper alloys in aquaculture</a>, copper alloys have become important netting materials in the <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> industry because they are <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobial</a> and prevent <a href="/wiki/Biofouling" title="Biofouling">biofouling</a>, even in extreme conditions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1995_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1995-110">[110]</a></sup> and have strong structural and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrosion-resistant" title="Corrosion-resistant">corrosion-resistant</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> properties in marine environments.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antimicrobial_applications">Antimicrobial applications</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_properties_of_copper" title="Antimicrobial properties of copper">Antimicrobial properties of copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_copper-alloy_touch_surfaces" title="Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces">Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_copper-alloy_touch_surfaces" title="Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces">Copper-alloy touch surfaces</a> have natural properties that destroy a wide range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microorganisms" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a> (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i> O157:H7, <a href="/wiki/Methicillin" title="Methicillin">methicillin</a>-resistant <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Methicillin-resistant_Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus">MRSA</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus" title="Staphylococcus">Staphylococcus</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clostridium_difficile_(bacteria)" title="Clostridium difficile (bacteria)">Clostridium difficile</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Influenza_A_virus" title="Influenza A virus">influenza A virus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adenoviridae" title="Adenoviridae">adenovirus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copper_Touch_Surfaces_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copper_Touch_Surfaces-112">[112]</a></sup> Some 355 copper alloys<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="please explain, what is a 355 copper alloy? (June 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> were proven to kill more than 99.9% of disease-causing bacteria within just two hours when cleaned regularly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa.gov_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-113">[113]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) has approved the registrations of these copper alloys as "<a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobial</a> materials with public health benefits";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa.gov_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-113">[113]</a></sup> that approval allows manufacturers to make legal claims to the public health benefits of products made of registered alloys. In addition, the EPA has approved a long list of antimicrobial copper products made from these alloys, such as bedrails, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handrails" title="Handrails">handrails</a>, over-bed tables, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinks" title="Sinks">sinks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faucets" title="Faucets">faucets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Door_knobs" title="Door knobs">door knobs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toilet" title="Toilet">toilet</a> hardware, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_keyboards" title="Computer keyboards">computer keyboards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health_club" title="Health club">health club</a> equipment, and <a href="/wiki/Shopping_cart" title="Shopping cart">shopping cart</a> handles (for a comprehensive list, see: <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_copper-alloy_touch_surfaces#Approved_products" title="Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces">Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces#Approved products</a>). Copper doorknobs are used by hospitals to reduce the transfer of disease, and <a href="/wiki/Legionnaires%27_disease" title="Legionnaires' disease">Legionnaires' disease</a> is suppressed by copper tubing in plumbing systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Antimicrobial copper alloy products are now being installed in healthcare facilities in the U.K., Ireland, Japan, Korea, France, Denmark, and Brazil<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and in the subway transit system in Santiago, Chile, where copper-zinc alloy handrails will be installed in some 30 stations between 2011 and 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_medicine">Folk medicine</span></h3>
<p>Copper is commonly used in jewelry, and according to some folklore, copper bracelets relieve <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">arthritis</a> symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>  In one trial for osteoarthritis and one trial for rheumatoid arthritis no differences is found between copper bracelet and control (non-copper) bracelet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichmondBrown2009_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-RichmondBrown2009-120">[120]</a></sup>  No evidence shows that copper can be absorbed through the skin. If it were, it might lead to <a href="/wiki/Copper_toxicity" title="Copper toxicity">copper poisoning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Compression_clothing">Compression clothing</span></h4>
<p>Recently, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compression_bandage" title="Compression bandage">compression</a> clothing with inter-woven copper has been marketed with health claims similar to the folk medicine claims. Because compression clothing is a valid treatment for some ailments, the clothing may have that benefit, but the added copper may have no benefit beyond a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placebo_effect" title="Placebo effect">placebo effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span></h3>
<p>Textile fibers are blended with copper to create antimicrobial protective fabrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ergowear_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ergowear-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Degradation">Degradation</span></h2>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Chromobacterium_violaceum" title="Chromobacterium violaceum">Chromobacterium violaceum</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_fluorescens" title="Pseudomonas fluorescens">Pseudomonas fluorescens</a></i> can both mobilize solid copper as a cyanide compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Michael_Gadd_609–643_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Michael_Gadd_609–643-125">[125]</a></sup> The ericoid mycorrhizal fungi associated with <i>Calluna</i>, <i>Erica</i> and <i>Vaccinium</i> can grow in metalliferous soils containing copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Michael_Gadd_609–643_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Michael_Gadd_609–643-125">[125]</a></sup> The ectomycorrhizal fungus <i>Suillus luteus</i> protects young pine trees from copper toxicity. A sample of the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus_niger" title="Aspergillus niger">Aspergillus niger</a></i> was found growing from gold mining solution and was found to contain cyano complexes of such metals as gold, silver, copper, iron, and zinc. The fungus also plays a role in the solubilization of heavy metal sulfides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_health" title="Copper in health">Copper in health</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1776" data-file-width="2700" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg/220px-ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg/330px-ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg/440px-ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rich sources of copper include oysters, beef and lamb liver, Brazil nuts, blackstrap molasses, cocoa, and black pepper. Good sources include lobster, nuts and sunflower seeds, green olives, avocados, and wheat bran.</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_proteins" title="Copper proteins">Copper proteins</a> have diverse roles in biological electron transport and oxygen transportation, processes that exploit the easy interconversion of Cu(I) and Cu(II).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Copper is essential in the aerobic <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a> of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotes" title="Eukaryotes">eukaryotes</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>, it is found in <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a>, which is the last protein in <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">oxidative phosphorylation</a>. Cytochrome c oxidase is the protein that binds the O<sub>2</sub> between a copper and an iron; the protein transfers 8 electrons to the O<sub>2</sub> molecule to reduce it to two molecules of water. Copper is also found in many <a href="/wiki/Superoxide_dismutase" title="Superoxide dismutase">superoxide dismutases</a>, proteins that catalyze the decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxides</a> by converting it (by <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionation</a>) to oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>Cu<sup>2+</sup>-SOD + O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> → Cu<sup>+</sup>-SOD + O<sub>2</sub> (reduction of copper; oxidation of superoxide)</li>
<li>Cu<sup>+</sup>-SOD + O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> + 2H<sup>+</sup> → Cu<sup>2+</sup>-SOD + H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (oxidation of copper; reduction of superoxide)</li></ul>
<p>The protein <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a> is the oxygen carrier in most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_crab" title="Horseshoe crab">horseshoe crab</a> (<i>Limulus polyphemus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-128">[128]</a></sup> Because hemocyanin is blue, these organisms have blue blood rather than the red blood of iron-based <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>. Structurally related to hemocyanin are the <a href="/wiki/Laccase" title="Laccase">laccases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tyrosinase" title="Tyrosinase">tyrosinases</a>. Instead of reversibly binding oxygen, these proteins hydroxylate substrates, illustrated by their role in the formation of <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">lacquers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lippard_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lippard-129">[129]</a></sup> The biological role for copper commenced with the appearance of oxygen in earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Several copper proteins, such as the "blue copper proteins", do not interact directly with substrates; hence they are not enzymes. These proteins relay electrons by the process called <a href="/wiki/Electron_transfer" title="Electron transfer">electron transfer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lippard_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lippard-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thylakoid_membrane.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="516" data-file-width="905" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thylakoid_membrane.png/440px-Thylakoid_membrane.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thylakoid_membrane.png/660px-Thylakoid_membrane.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thylakoid_membrane.png/880px-Thylakoid_membrane.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thylakoid_membrane.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photosynthesis functions by an elaborate electron transport chain within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thylakoid_membrane" title="Thylakoid membrane">thylakoid membrane</a>. A central link in this chain is <a href="/wiki/Plastocyanin" title="Plastocyanin">plastocyanin</a>, a blue copper protein.</div></div></div>
<p>A unique tetranuclear copper center has been found in <a href="/wiki/Nitrous-oxide_reductase" title="Nitrous-oxide reductase">nitrous-oxide reductase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chemical compounds which were developed for treatment of Wilson's disease have been investigated for use in cancer therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_needs">Dietary needs</span></h3>
<p>Copper is an essential <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace element</a> in plants and animals, but not all microorganisms. The human body contains copper at a level of about 1.4 to 2.1 mg per kg of body mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-copper.org_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-copper.org-133">[133]</a></sup> Copper is absorbed in the gut, then transported to the liver bound to <a href="/wiki/Serum_albumin" title="Serum albumin">albumin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> After processing in the liver, copper is distributed to other tissues in a second phase, which involves the protein <a href="/wiki/Ceruloplasmin" title="Ceruloplasmin">ceruloplasmin</a>, carrying the majority of copper in blood. Ceruloplasmin also carries the copper that is excreted in milk, and is particularly well-absorbed as a copper source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Copper in the body normally undergoes <a href="/wiki/Enterohepatic_circulation" title="Enterohepatic circulation">enterohepatic circulation</a> (about 5 mg a day, vs. about 1 mg per day absorbed in the diet and excreted from the body), and the body is able to excrete some excess copper, if needed, via <a href="/wiki/Bile" title="Bile">bile</a>, which carries some copper out of the liver that is not then reabsorbed by the intestine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_recommendations">Dietary recommendations</span></h3>
<p>The U.S. Institute of Medicine (IOM) updated the estimated average requirements (EARs) and recommended dietary allowances (RDAs) for copper in 2001. If there is not sufficient information to establish EARs and RDAs, an estimate designated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adequate_Intake" title="Adequate Intake">Adequate Intake</a> (AI) is used instead. The AIs for copper are: 200 μg of copper for 0–6-month-old males and females, and 220 μg of copper for 7–12-month-old males and females. The RDAs for copper are: 340 μg of copper for 1–3-year-old males, 440 μg of copper for 4–8-year-old males, 700 μg of copper for 9–13-year-old males, 890 μg of copper for 14–18-year-old males, and 900 μg of copper for males that are 19 years old and older. The RDAs for copper are: 340 μg of copper for 1–3-year-old females, 440 μg of copper for 4–8-year-old females, 700 μg of copper for 9–13-year-old females, 890 μg of copper for 14–18-year-old females, and 900 μg of copper for females that are 19 years old and older. The RDAs for copper are: 1,000 μg of copper for 14–50-year-old pregnant females; furthermore, 1,300 μg of copper for 14–50-year-old lactating females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> As for safety, the IOM also sets <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolerable_upper_intake_level" title="Tolerable upper intake level">Tolerable upper intake levels</a> (ULs) for vitamins and minerals when evidence is sufficient. In the case of copper the UL is set at 10 mg/day. Collectively the EARs, RDAs, AIs and ULs are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Dietary Reference Intakes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA) refers to the collective set of information as Dietary Reference Values, with Population Reference Intake (PRI) instead of RDA, and Average Requirement instead of EAR. AI and UL defined the same as in United States. For women and men ages 18 and older the AIs are set at 1.3 and 1.6 mg/day, respectively. AIs for pregnancy and lactation is 1.5 mg/day. For children ages 1–17 years the AIs increase with age from 0.7 to 1.3 mg/day. These AIs are higher than the U.S. RDAs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The European Food Safety Authority reviewed the same safety question and set its UL at 5 mg/day, which is half the U.S. value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>For U.S. food and dietary supplement labeling purposes the amount in a serving is expressed as a percent of Daily Value (%DV). For copper labeling purposes 100% of the Daily Value was 2.0 mg, but as of May 27, 2016 it was revised to 0.9 mg to bring it into agreement with the RDA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedReg_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FedReg-142">[142]</a></sup> A table of the old and new adult Daily Values is provided at <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake" title="Reference Daily Intake">Reference Daily Intake</a>. The original deadline to be in compliance was July 28, 2018, but on September 29, 2017 the FDA released a proposed rule that extended the deadline to January 1, 2020 for large companies and January 1, 2021 for small companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FDAdelay_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FDAdelay-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deficiency">Deficiency</span></h3>
<p>Because of its role in facilitating iron uptake, <a href="/wiki/Copper_deficiency" title="Copper deficiency">copper deficiency</a> can produce <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>-like symptoms, <a href="/wiki/Neutropenia" title="Neutropenia">neutropenia</a>, bone abnormalities, hypopigmentation, impaired growth, increased incidence of infections, osteoporosis, hyperthyroidism, and abnormalities in glucose and cholesterol metabolism. Conversely, <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_disease" title="Wilson's disease">Wilson's disease</a> causes an accumulation of copper in body tissues.
</p><p>Severe deficiency can be found by testing for low plasma or serum copper levels, low ceruloplasmin, and low red blood cell superoxide dismutase levels; these are not sensitive to marginal copper status. The "cytochrome c oxidase activity of leucocytes and platelets" has been stated as another factor in deficiency, but the results have not been confirmed by replication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonhametal2002_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonhametal2002-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Copper_toxicity" title="Copper toxicity">Copper toxicity</a></div>
<p>Gram quantities of various copper salts have been taken in suicide attempts and produced acute copper toxicity in humans, possibly due to redox cycling and the generation of <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> that damage <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Corresponding amounts of copper salts (30 mg/kg) are toxic in animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> A minimum dietary value for healthy growth in rabbits has been reported to be at least 3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> in the diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> However, higher concentrations of copper (100 ppm, 200 ppm, or 500 ppm) in the diet of rabbits may favorably influence <a href="/wiki/Feed_conversion_ratio" title="Feed conversion ratio">feed conversion efficiency</a>, growth rates, and carcass dressing percentages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chronic copper toxicity does not normally occur in humans because of transport systems that regulate absorption and excretion. Autosomal recessive mutations in copper transport proteins can disable these systems, leading to <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_disease" title="Wilson's disease">Wilson's disease</a> with copper accumulation and <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a> of the liver in persons who have inherited two defective genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-copper.org_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-copper.org-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elevated copper levels have also been linked to worsening symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_exposure">Human exposure</span></h3>
<p>In the US, the <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has designated a <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a> (PEL) for copper dust and fumes in the workplace as a time-weighted average (TWA) of 1 mg/m<sup>3</sup>. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">Recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 1 mg/m<sup>3</sup>, time-weighted average. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">IDLH</a> (immediately dangerous to life and health) value is 100 mg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper is a constituent of <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoke" title="Tobacco smoke">tobacco smoke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TalhoutSchulz2011_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-TalhoutSchulz2011-154">[154]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tobacco_plant" title="Tobacco plant">tobacco plant</a> readily absorbs and accumulates <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, such as copper from the surrounding soil into its leaves. These are readily absorbed into the user's body following smoke inhalation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The health implications are not clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
